# Incident I00245: Fake videos, AI deepfakes and hacked accounts: No truce in India-Pakistan disinformation war

* **Summary:** <i>In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.</i>

* **incident type**: 

* **Year started:** 

* **Countries:**  , 

* **Found via:** 

* **Date added:** 


| Reference | Pub Date | Authors | Org | Archive |
| --------- | -------- | ------- | --- | ------- |
| [https://www.malaymail.com/news/world/2025/05/13/fake-videos-ai-deepfakes-and-hacked-accounts-no-truce-in-india-pakistan-disinformation-war/176645](https://www.malaymail.com/news/world/2025/05/13/fake-videos-ai-deepfakes-and-hacked-accounts-no-truce-in-india-pakistan-disinformation-war/176645) | 2025-05-13 00:00:00 | AFP | Malaymail | [https://archive.is/9i1qt](https://archive.is/9i1qt) |

 

| Technique | Description given for this incident |
| --------- | ------------------------- |
| [T0039.001 Collaborating Assets Seed and Ping](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0039.001.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0039.002 Solicit Production of Fact Check](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0039.002.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0048.005 Voyeuristic Content](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0048.005.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0068 Respond to Breaking News Event or Active Crisis](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0068.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0068 Respond to Breaking News Event or Active Crisis](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0068.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0160.005 Content Produced as Satire](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0160.005.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

| [T0162.003 Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.003.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0162.003 Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.003.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0162.003 Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.003.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0162.003 Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.003.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0162.005 Video Game Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Reality](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.005.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have announced a ceasefire after coming close to all-out conflict, but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military shared footage that had previously circulated in reports about a 2023 Israeli air strike in Gaza (T0162.003: Historic Content Incorrectly Presented as Current, T0162.004: Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Another Location).<br><br>The clip quickly appeared on television (T0117: Attract Traditional Media) and social media but was later retracted by numerous media outlets, including AFP.<br><br>AI-generated imagery has also muddied the waters (T0166: AI-Generated Content), including a video that purportedly shows a Pakistan Army general saying the country lost two of its aircraft (T0166.001: Deepfake Impersonation, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0161.002: Statement Incorrectly Presented as Made by Individual or Institution).<br><br>AFP fact-checkers found the clip was altered from a 2024 press conference (T0162.008: Context Reframed by Edits to Media).<br><br>“We have seen a new wave of AI-based content in both video and still images due to increased access to deepfake tools,” said Joyojeet Pal, an associate professor in the School of Information at the University of Michigan.<br><br>Cyber alert, social media crackdown<br><br>Both India and Pakistan have taken advantage of the information vacuum to raise alarm bells and promote their own claims and counter-claims.<br><br>Pakistan appears to have lifted a more than one-year-old ban on X the same day of the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |
| [T0162.010 Entertainment Media Content Incorrectly Presented as Depicting Reality](../../generated_pages/techniques/T0162.010.md) | <i>NEW DELHI, May 13 — In Islamabad India and Pakistan have crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

the Indian strikes, according to an AFP analysis of data from the nonprofit Open Observatory of Network Interference.<br><br>“In a time of crisis, the government needed its people’s voice to be heard all around the world and not to be silenced anymore like it was before for domestic political purposes,” said Usama Khilji, a digital rights expert and activist in Pakistan.<br><br>The country’s National Cyber Emergency Response Team (NCERT) on May 8 issued an alert about “increased cyberattacks and misinformation via emails, social media, QR codes, and messaging apps”.<br><br>Both Pakistan’s Ministry of Economic Affairs and the Karachi Port Trust later said their X accounts had been hacked.<br><br>A post from the latter account said the port — one of South Asia’s busiest — was attacked by the Indian military (T0146: Account Asset, T0097.111: Government Official Persona, T0150.005: Compromised Asset, T0145.005: Compromised Persona).<br><br>The page was later restored and the port authority said no attack had taken place.<br><br>India, meanwhile, has executed a sweeping crackdown targeting the social media accounts of Pakistani politicians, celebrities and media organisations (T0124.001: Report Non-Violative Opposing Content).<br><br>The government ordered X to block more than 8,000 accounts and banned more than a dozen Pakistani YouTube channels for allegedly spreading “provocative” content, including news outlets.</i> |

but on social media citizens on both sides are vying to control public perceptions by peddling disinformation.<br><br>[...]<br><br>After the first round of Indian air strikes, the Pakistani military
